      <p><i><b><a href="/wiki/Salvia_divinorum" title="Salvia divinorum">Salvia divinorum</a></b></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Psychoactive" class="mw-redirect" title="Psychoactive">psychoactive</a> plant, is legal in most countries. Exceptions, countries where there is some form of control, include <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> and in 33 states and territories of the <a href="/wiki/Legal_status_of_Salvia_divinorum_in_the_United_States" title="Legal status of Salvia divinorum in the United States">United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SiebertLegalStatus_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SiebertLegalStatus-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ErowidLegalStatus_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ErowidLegalStatus-2">[2]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the United Kingdom, following a local newspaper story in October 2005,<sup id="cite_ref-Worksop20051014_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Worksop20051014-3">[3]</a></sup> a parliamentary <a href="/wiki/Early_Day_Motion" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Day Motion">Early Day Motion</a> was raised calling for <i>Salvia divinorum</i> to be banned there. However, it only received 11 signatures.<sup id="cite_ref-Mann2005_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mann2005-4">[4]</a></sup> A second Early Day Motion was raised in October 2008 attracting 18 signatures.<sup id="cite_ref-Early_day_motion_2235_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Early_day_motion_2235-5">[5]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Advisory_Council_on_the_Misuse_of_Drugs" title="Advisory Council on the Misuse of Drugs">Advisory Council on the Misuse of Drugs</a>, the independent body that advises UK government on drugs, has been asked to investigate further.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> On the 28 January 2016, the <a href="/wiki/Psychoactive_Substances_Act_2016" title="Psychoactive Substances Act 2016">Psychoactive Substances Act 2016</a> was passed. The act came into force on 26 May 2016, across the entire United Kingdom, making <i>salvia</i> illegal to possess with intent to supply, possess on custodial premises, supply, import for human consumption, or produce for human consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-Psychoactive_Substances_Act_2016_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Psychoactive_Substances_Act_2016-7">[7]</a></sup> The two sponsors for the bill were <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a> <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> member <a href="/wiki/Michael_Bates,_Baron_Bates" title="Michael Bates, Baron Bates">Michael Bates</a> and Conservative <a href="/wiki/Member_of_parliament" title="Member of parliament">MP</a> <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In such places where <i>Salvia divinorum</i> legislation exists, it varies in prohibitive degree from country to country. Australia has imposed its strictest 'Schedule 9' (US Schedule I equivalent or equivalent to class A in the UK) classification, for example, and Italy has also placed Salvia in its 'Table I' of controlled substances (also US Schedule I and class A equivalent). In Spain there are just controls focusing on the commercial trade of <i>Salvia divinorum</i>, and private cultivation (growing one's own plants for non-commercial use) is not targeted. </p>
      <p>In the United States, Salvia is not regulated under the <a href="/wiki/Controlled_Substances_Act" title="Controlled Substances Act">Controlled Substances Act</a> but some states, including Delaware, Illinois, Louisiana, Missouri, Virginia, Tennessee, Texas, and others, have passed their own laws.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Several other states have proposed legislation against Salvia, including Alabama, Alaska, California, Florida, Georgia, Iowa, New Jersey, New York, Ohio, Oregon, and Pennsylvania. Many of these proposals have not made it into law, with motions having failed, stalled or otherwise died, for example at committee review stages.<sup id="cite_ref-SiebertLegalStatus_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SiebertLegalStatus-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ErowidLegalStatus_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ErowidLegalStatus-2">[2]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>National legislation for amendment of the Controlled Substances Act to place salvinorin A and <i>Salvia divinorum</i> in Schedule I at the federal level was proposed in 2002 by <a href="/wiki/Rep._Joe_Baca" class="mw-redirect" title="Rep. Joe Baca">Representative Joe Baca</a> (<a href="/wiki/United_States_Democratic_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Democratic Party">D</a>- California). Those opposed to bill HR 5607 include <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Siebert_(ethnobotanist)" title="Daniel Siebert (ethnobotanist)">Daniel Siebert</a>, who sent a letter to Congress arguing against the proposed legislation,<sup id="cite_ref-Siebert2002_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Siebert2002-10">[10]</a></sup> and the Center for Cognitive Liberty &amp; Ethics (CCLE), who sent key members of the US Congress a report on <i>Salvia divinorum</i> and its active principle,<sup id="cite_ref-Boire2002_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boire2002-11">[11]</a></sup> along with letters from an array of scientists who expressed concern that scheduling <i>Salvia divinorum</i> would negatively impact important research on the plant. The bill did not pass.<sup id="cite_ref-Baca2002_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baca2002-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CCLE2002_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CCLE2002-14">[14]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Salvia cultivation may prove difficult to police. The plant has a nondescript appearance; unlike cannabis the leaves are not distinctive and it does not have a distinctive odour. <i>Salvia divinorum</i> looks like and can be grown as an ordinary <a href="/wiki/Houseplant" title="Houseplant">houseplant</a> without the need of special equipment such as <a href="/wiki/Hydroponic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydroponic">hydroponics</a> or high-power lights.<sup id="cite_ref-Shulgin2003_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shulgin2003-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chalmers20060506_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers20060506-16">[16]</a></sup> </p> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886049878">.mw-parser-output .tocright{float:right;clear:right;width:auto;background:none;padding:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em;margin-bottom:.5em}.mw-parser-output .tocright-clear-left{clear:left}.mw-parser-output .tocright-clear-both{clear:both}.mw-parser-output .tocright-clear-none{clear:none}</style>
      <div class="tocright" style="">
       <div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading">
        <input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none">

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Opinions_and_arguments">Opinions and arguments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Legal_status_of_Salvia_divinorum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Opinions and arguments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>Concerns expressed by some politicians on the subject of Salvia echo those of the media. In November 2006, the morning after a story by news channel KSL was aired in Utah, warning its viewers about what it called "this dangerous herb",<sup id="cite_ref-Dujanovic20061127_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dujanovic20061127-17">[17]</a></sup> Utah State Representative <a href="/wiki/Paul_Ray" title="Paul Ray">Paul Ray</a> (R) submitted a bill calling for its Schedule I classification in that state. KSL TV cameras were on Capitol Hill to see the paperwork filed, with KSL reporting - "Moments after our story ended, Utah Representative Paul Ray began writing a bill to ban Salvia." As he presented the bill Ray said - "It was upsetting to see we have a drug of that strength that's legal." and "We're basically going to make it illegal to possess or sell. Period."<sup id="cite_ref-Dujanovic20061128_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dujanovic20061128-18">[18]</a></sup> Ray's action was further supported by the news channel in a subsequent KSL editorial. Viewer feedback was unanimously more critical.<sup id="cite_ref-Cardall20061128_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cardall20061128-19">[19]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Georgia State Senator John Bulloch (R) reportedly saw a report on an Atlanta television news station about the increased use of <i>Salvia divinorum</i>. He was quoted as saying - "I thought, 'Why hasn't somebody already jumped on this?" before filing Senate Bill 295. "I hurriedly got legislative counsel to draft the bill.... Everything that I read about it is it's considered to be a hallucinogenic drug.... A lot of the reading that I've found on it says that it gives a quicker and more intense high than LSD." Senator Don Thomas (R) was reported as saying -"I just know about the publicity of the dangers of it, and the use of it, so my first impression is to ban anything of that nature."<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In February 2007, the day after a Fox TV local news story on Salvia had aired in Milwaukee,<sup id="cite_ref-Sanchick20070214_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sanchick20070214-21">[21]</a></sup> Wisconsin state lawmaker <a href="/wiki/Sheldon_Wasserman" title="Sheldon Wasserman">Sheldon Wasserman</a> (D), also a licensed physician, who had never heard of it before, spoke to Fox news in a follow-up report about then wanting to make it a Schedule I controlled substance.<sup id="cite_ref-Sanchick20070215_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sanchick20070215-22">[22]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Comparisons to LSD and particular focus on "protecting our children" are also echoed by politicians. In June 2007 the Wisconsin State Journal newspaper ran a front-page headline cover story about Salvia, reporting that Representative Wasserman had recently begun seeking sponsors for a bill that would ban the manufacture and sale of <i>Salvia divinorum</i> for consumption in Wisconsin. Dr. Wasserman was reported as saying - "This bill is all about protecting our children" and "I want to stop the Salvia divinorum dealers who are pushing young people to experiment with a potentially dangerous substance."<sup id="cite_ref-Martell20070618_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martell20070618-23">[23]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In connection with his proposals to make <i>Salvia divinorum</i> and salvinorin A Schedule I controlled substances in Oregon, Representative <a href="/wiki/John_Lim" title="John Lim">John Lim</a> (R) was quoted as saying - "From what I understand this drug is at least as dangerous as marijuana or LSD", and Seth Hatmaker, a spokesman for Lim - "I think it's only a matter of time before we find people addicted to this stuff".<sup id="cite_ref-Clark20070305_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark20070305-24">[24]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the state of Illinois, in support of his bill for Schedule I classification of <i>Salvia divinorum</i>, Representative <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Reboletti" title="Dennis Reboletti">Dennis Reboletti</a> (R) wrote in his own website that Salvia is a "powerful psychoactive plant which in appearance looks like marijuana but has the psychoactive properties of LSD." and "It's important that we in the legislature are proactive in protecting our children from highly addictive substances" [...] "For a drug to be classified as a Schedule 1 substance signifies that it's a highly dangerous and potentially lethal drug for its user. Hopefully, the passage of my bill will bring attention to "Magic Mint" and help law enforcement combat the future rise of this drug."<sup id="cite_ref-Reboletti2007Mar_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reboletti2007Mar-25">[25]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Other references and sources indicate however that <i>Salvia divinorum</i> does not look like marijuana. Its psychoactive properties are not like those of LSD, and that Salvia divinorum is not generally understood to be either addictive or toxic. </p>
      <p>Concerns about driving while under the influence of Salvia have also been expressed. Delaware State Senator Karen Peterson (D), who introduced Schedule I classification of <i>Salvia divinorum</i> in Delaware, said - "I, for one, don't want to be driving down Route 1 next to someone who is having an out-of-body experience"<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> and "I thought this is not something that I would want people using driving around the streets of Delaware."<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>There has not been any evidence to suggest that Salvia use is problematic. Some "arguments" against Salvia have been of a preventative or imitative nature. North Dakota State Senator Randy Christmann (R) stated - "we need to stop this before it gets to be a huge problem not after it gets to be a huge problem"<sup id="cite_ref-KXMBTV20070131_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KXMBTV20070131-28">[28]</a></sup> and New Jersey Assemblyman <a href="/wiki/Jack_Conners" title="Jack Conners">Jack Conners</a> (D) argued -"Salvia divinorum use may not be a runway epidemic, but it's certainly is a phenomenon that warrants attention. We should take preventive steps now to prevent wholesale problems later on"<sup id="cite_ref-Teel2006_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Teel2006-29">[29]</a></sup> In October 2005 MP <a href="/wiki/John_Mann_(British_politician)" class="mw-redirect" title="John Mann (British politician)">John Mann</a> raised an ultimately unsuccessful <a href="/wiki/Early_Day_Motion" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Day Motion">Early Day Motion</a> calling for <i>Salvia divinorum</i> to be banned in the UK, saying - "The Australians have clearly found a problem with it. There's obviously a risk in people taking it."<sup id="cite_ref-Worksop20051014_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Worksop20051014-3">[3]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The National Institute on Money in State Politics indicates the major sources of campaign contributions for US politicians. For example, Oregon State Representative John Lim's largest individual campaign sponsor in 2006 was the Oregon Beer &amp; Wine Distributors Association. Lim argued for Schedule I classification of Salvia in Oregon. Senator Karen Peterson's second largest group campaign donations in 2006 came from 'Beer, Wine &amp; Liquor' industries. Peterson introduced Schedule I classification of <i>Salvia divinorum</i> in Delaware. Tennessee State Senator <a href="/wiki/Tim_Burchett" title="Tim Burchett">Tim Burchett</a> (R) sponsored Salvia legislation in Tennessee. In 2006 his second largest individual campaign donation came from the Tennessee Malt Beverage Association. In the same period alcohol and tobacco related contributions amounted to the fourth largest industry contributions for Representative Paul Ray in Utah. Alcohol related contributions also featured highly for Representative Dennis Reboletti in Illinois - 'Beer, Wine &amp; Liquor' was his seventh highest industry contributor.<sup id="cite_ref-MiSP2006_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MiSP2006-30">[30]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Opponents of more prohibitive measures against Salvia argue that such reactions are largely due to an inherent prejudice and a particular cultural bias rather than any actual balance of evidence, pointing out inconsistencies in attitudes toward other more toxic and addictive drugs such as <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_(drug)" title="Alcohol (drug)">alcohol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nicotine" title="Nicotine">nicotine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The worldwide number of alcohol-related deaths is calculated at over 2,000 people per day,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> in the US the number is over 300 deaths per day.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> While not objecting to some form of legal control, in particular with regard to the sale to minors or sale of enhanced high-strength extracts, most Salvia proponents otherwise argue against stricter legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-SiebertLegalStatus_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SiebertLegalStatus-1">[1]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Those advocating consideration of <i>Salvia divinorum'</i>s potential for beneficial use in a modern context have two major arguments: First that Salvia Divinorum is a potent kappa opioid agonist and given its activity and modulatory effect on the <a href="/wiki/Kappa_opioid_receptor" class="mw-redirect" title="Kappa opioid receptor">kappa opioid receptor</a>, and therefore similarity of its profile of effect to <a href="/wiki/Ibogaine" title="Ibogaine">ibogaine</a>, (used successfully to treat addiction around the world) that further research into its potential as a potent anti-addiction medication<sup id="cite_ref-pmid15542743_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15542743-34">[34]</a></sup> will be hindered by careless lawmaking. Secondly, advocates argue that more could be learned from Mazatec culture, where Salvia is not really associated with notions of drug taking at all and it is rather considered as a spiritual sacrament. In light of this it is argued that <i>Salvia divinorum</i> could be better understood more positively as an <a href="/wiki/Entheogen" title="Entheogen">entheogen</a> rather than pejoratively as a <a href="/wiki/Hallucinogen" title="Hallucinogen">hallucinogen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BlosserLessons_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BlosserLessons-35">[35]</a></sup> Other entheogenic plants with continuing traditions principally of spiritual use include <a href="/wiki/Peyote" title="Peyote">peyote</a> (and other psychoactive cacti), <a href="/wiki/Iboga" class="mw-redirect" title="Iboga">iboga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virola" title="Virola">virola</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ayahuasca" title="Ayahuasca">ayahuasca</a> (an admixture of plants containing <a href="/wiki/Dimethyltryptamine" class="mw-redirect" title="Dimethyltryptamine">DMT</a> + <a href="/wiki/MAOI" class="mw-redirect" title="MAOI">MAOI</a>), and various types of psychoactive fungi.<sup id="cite_ref-OtherEntheogenicPlants_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OtherEntheogenicPlants-36">[36]</a></sup> In fact, US legislation as it stands specifically allows two of these to be used in a spiritual context. The <a href="/wiki/Native_American_Church" title="Native American Church">Native American Church</a> is allowed to use peyote and <a href="/wiki/Uni%C3%A3o_do_Vegetal" title="Uniรฃo do Vegetal">Uniรฃo do Vegetal</a> (or UDV) is permitted ayahuasca.<sup id="cite_ref-USLegislation_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USLegislation-37">[37]</a></sup> Although not consistently granted (varying from state to state), the principal grounds for such concessions are constitutional,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> with further grounds following from the <a href="/wiki/Religious_Freedom_Restoration_Act" title="Religious Freedom Restoration Act">Religious Freedom Restoration Act</a>. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="List_by_country">List by country</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Legal_status_of_Salvia_divinorum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: List by country">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

         <td><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Flag_of_Austria.svg/23px-Flag_of_Austria.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Flag_of_Austria.svg/35px-Flag_of_Austria.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Flag_of_Austria.svg/45px-Flag_of_Austria.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600">&nbsp;</span>Austria</td> 
         <td style="background:#9F9;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-yes">Legal</td> 
         <td style="background:#9F9;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-yes">Legal</td> 
         <td style="background:#9F9;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-yes">Legal</td> 
         <td style="background:#9F9;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-yes">Legal</td> 
         <td>Salvia divinorum is completely legal in Austria without any restrictions. The supreme court of Austria decided that Salvia Divinorum is not affected by the "Neue Psychoaktive Substanzen" act, which bans the sale and possession of research chemicals for the purpose of human consumption. The court ruled that the law only includes chemical, unnatural substances.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr>

         <td style="background:#F99;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-no">Illegal</td> 
         <td style="background:#F99;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-no">Illegal</td> 
         <td>As of 1 June 2002, Australia became the first country to ban Salvia and salvinorin. According to the Australian Drugs and Poisons Committee, salvia had not yet shown evidence of damage or threat to public health/safety but had potential to be abused. In a statement which has been criticized as self-negating the committee said, "there was no evidence of traditional therapeutic use other than in shamanistic healing rituals".<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> On 1 June 2008, the state of Queensland made Salvia divinorum a schedule 2 dangerous drug, in the same category as cannabis sativa and GHB.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </td>
        </tr>

         <td>In October 2004, "salvorin A", a misspelling of salvinorin A, was added to the Belgian list of illegal products, so the law was actually banning a non-existent substance.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> <p>Two years later, in October 2006, the mistake was corrected, and the whole Salvia divinorum plant was made explicitly illegal.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> </p> </td>

         <td>With effect from 23 August 2003, <i>Salvia divinorum</i> and salvinorin A were classed as 'category B' drugs in Danish law. Category B includes psilocybin mushrooms, cocaine, amphetamine, and several others substances that are only legal for medicinal and scientific purposes.          <td><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Flag_of_New_Zealand.svg/23px-Flag_of_New_Zealand.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Flag_of_New_Zealand.svg/35px-Flag_of_New_Zealand.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Flag_of_New_Zealand.svg/46px-Flag_of_New_Zealand.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="600">&nbsp;</span>New Zealand</td> 
         <td style="background:#9F9;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-yes">Legal<br>(Unless for human consumption)</td> 
         <td style="background:#9F9;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-yes">Legal<br>(Unless for human consumption)</td> 
         <td style="background:#9F9;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-yes">Legal<br>(Unless for human consumption)</td> 
         <td style="background:#9F9;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-yes">Legal<br>(Unless for human consumption)</td> 
         <td>In November 2007 New Zealand National party MP <a href="/wiki/Jacqui_Dean" title="Jacqui Dean">Jacqui Dean</a> called for the government to take action, saying - "Salvia Divinorum is a hallucinogenic drug, which has been banned in Australia, and yet here in New Zealand it continues to be sold freely." and "Weโre dealing with a dangerous drug here, with the minister's wait and see approach like playing Russian Roulette with young people's lives."<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> <p>Jacqui Dean has similar concerns about the 'party pill' <a href="/wiki/Benzylpiperazine" title="Benzylpiperazine">benzylpiperazine</a> (BZP), over which Associate Health Minister <a href="/wiki/Jim_Anderton" title="Jim Anderton">Jim Anderton</a> (<a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Progressive_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="New Zealand Progressive Party">Progressive party</a>) has accused her of indulging in political grandstanding, saying - "Perhaps Mrs Dean doesn't subscribe to the idea that any Government must balance the need to act promptly with its responsibilities to act fairly and follow due process, particularly where its actions affect those who are currently acting within existing legal constraints."<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> When questioned by <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_Party" title="Mฤori Party">Mฤori Party</a> MP <a href="/wiki/Tariana_Turia" title="Tariana Turia">Tariana Turia</a>, on why she was unwilling to take the same prohibitory line on smoking cigarettes and drinking alcohol as she took on BZP Ms Dean said "Alcohol and tobacco have been with our society for many, many years."<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> In September 2007, the Social Tonics Association of New Zealand (STANZ) called for Jacqui Dean to step down from speaking on drug issues after she demonstrated "a lack of credibility in calling for the <a href="/wiki/Dihydrogen_monoxide_hoax" class="mw-redirect" title="Dihydrogen monoxide hoax">ban of dihydrogen monoxide</a> (H<sub>2</sub>O - i.e. water.)" STANZ Chairman Matt Bowden said - "The DHMO hoax played on the member this week is not a joke, it highlights a serious issue at the heart of drug policy making. Ms Dean demonstrated a โban anything moderately harmfulโ reflex. This approach is just downright dangerous." - "Jacqui Dean has clearly demonstrated a lack of credibility in her requests to the Minister to consider banning water; She has also seriously embarrassed her National Party colleagues who can no longer have confidence in her petitions to ban BZP or anything else."<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> </p><p>As of 18 July 2013 Salvia is illegal to sell in New Zealand without a license under the Psychoactive Substances Act 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-Law_and_Penalties_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law_and_Penalties-62">[62]</a></sup> This act is designed to prohibit any psychoactive substances which have not been approved by the Psychoactive Substances Regulatory Authority.<sup id="cite_ref-Psychoactive_Substances_Bill_100-3_(2013)_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Psychoactive_Substances_Bill_100-3_(2013)-63">[63]</a></sup> </p> </td>
        </tr>

         <td><a href="/wiki/Riksdag" title="Riksdag"><i>Sveriges riksdags</i></a> health ministry <a href="https://sv.wikipedia.org/wiki/Statens_folkh%C3%A4lsoinstitut" class="extiw" title="sv:Statens folkhรคlsoinstitut"><i>Statens folkhรคlsoinstitut</i></a> classified <i><a href="/wiki/Salvia_divinorum" title="Salvia divinorum">Salvia divinorum</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Salvinorin_A" title="Salvinorin A">Salvinorin A</a> as "health hazard" under the act <a href="https://sv.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lagen_om_f%C3%B6rbud_mot_vissa_h%C3%A4lsofarliga_varor" class="extiw" title="sv:Lagen om fรถrbud mot vissa hรคlsofarliga varor"><i>Lagen om fรถrbud mot vissa hรคlsofarliga varor</i></a> (translated <i>Act on the Prohibition of Certain Goods Dangerous to Health</i>) as of 1 April 2006, in their regulation <i>SFS 2006:167</i> listed as <i>alla delar av vรคxten Salvia divinorum frรฅn vilka salvinorin A inte blivit extraherat och oavsett under vilka benรคmningar de fรถrekommer</i>, making it illegal to sell or possess.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Switzerland.svg/16px-Flag_of_Switzerland.svg.png" decoding="async" width="16" height="16" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Switzerland.svg/24px-Flag_of_Switzerland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Switzerland.svg/32px-Flag_of_Switzerland.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512">&nbsp;&nbsp;</span>Switzerland</td> 
         <td style="background:#F99;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-no">Illegal</td> 
         <td style="background:#F99;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-no">Illegal</td>

         <td style="background:#F99;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-no">Illegal</td> 
         <td>Illegal since 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> </td>

         <td><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Thailand.svg/23px-Flag_of_Thailand.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Thailand.svg/35px-Flag_of_Thailand.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Thailand.svg/45px-Flag_of_Thailand.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600">&nbsp;</span>Thailand</td> 
         <td style="background:#9F9;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-yes">Legal</td> 
         <td style="background:#9F9;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-yes">Legal</td> 
         <td style="background:#9F9;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-yes">Legal</td> 
         <td style="background:#9F9;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-yes">Legal</td> 
         <td>The law in <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> does not prohibit salvia <sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> </td>

         <td><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Flag_of_Ukraine.svg/23px-Flag_of_Ukraine.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Flag_of_Ukraine.svg/35px-Flag_of_Ukraine.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Flag_of_Ukraine.svg/45px-Flag_of_Ukraine.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800">&nbsp;</span>Ukraine</td> 
         <td style="background:#F99;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-no">Illegal</td> 
         <td style="background:#F99;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-no">Illegal</td> 
         <td style="background:#F99;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-no">Illegal</td> 
         <td style="background:#F99;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-no">Illegal</td> 
         <td>Illegal since 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> </td>

        <tr> 
         <td><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="600">&nbsp;</span>United Kingdom</td> 
         <td style="background:#FFB;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-partial"><br>Illegal to possess with intent to supply or possess on custodial premises</td> 
         <td style="background:#F99;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-no">Illegal<br>(to supply for human consumption)</td> 
         <td style="background:#F99;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-no">Illegal<br>(to import for human consumption)</td> 
         <td style="background:#F99;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-no">Illegal<br>(to produce for human consumption)</td> 
         <td>In September 2001, in answer to a <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">parliamentary</a> question from <a href="/wiki/Ann_Widdecombe" title="Ann Widdecombe">Ann Widdecombe</a> <a href="/wiki/Member_of_parliament" title="Member of parliament">MP</a>, asking the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_the_Home_Office" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of State for the Home Office">Secretary of State for the Home Office</a> "what plans he has to review the legal status of the hallucinogen <i>Salvia divinorum</i>", <a href="/wiki/Bob_Ainsworth" title="Bob Ainsworth">Bob Ainsworth</a>, a parliamentary Under-Secretary for the UK Home Office, stated that "The Government are not aware of any evidence of significant misuse of this plant and have no current plans to review its legal status".<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> <p>Following a local newspaper story in October 2005,<sup id="cite_ref-worksopguardian.co.uk_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-worksopguardian.co.uk-73">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bassetlaw_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Bassetlaw (UK Parliament constituency)">Bassetlaw</a> MP <a href="/wiki/John_Mann_(British_politician)" class="mw-redirect" title="John Mann (British politician)">John Mann</a> raised an <a href="/wiki/Early_Day_Motion" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Day Motion">Early Day Motion</a> calling for <i>Salvia divinorum</i> to be banned in the UK. (EDM796).<sup id="cite_ref-worksopguardian.co.uk_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-worksopguardian.co.uk-73">[73]</a></sup> The motion only received 11 signatures.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> A second motion raised in October 2008 attracted 18 signatures.<sup id="cite_ref-Early_day_motion_2235_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Early_day_motion_2235-5">[5]</a></sup> </p><p>As of 25 May 2016, the <a href="/wiki/Psychoactive_Substances_Act_2016" title="Psychoactive Substances Act 2016">Psychoactive Substances Act 2016</a> makes <i>salvia</i> illegal to possess with intent to supply, possess on custodial premises, supply, import for human consumption, or produce for human consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-Psychoactive_Substances_Act_2016_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Psychoactive_Substances_Act_2016-7">[7]</a></sup> The act was introduced by <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> member <a href="/wiki/Michael_Bates,_Baron_Bates" title="Michael Bates, Baron Bates">Michael Bates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Member_of_parliament" title="Member of parliament">MP</a> <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> </p> </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1235" data-file-height="650">&nbsp;</span>United States</td>
